</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:General_geometry" title="Template:General geometry"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:General_geometry" title="Template talk:General geometry"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:General_geometry&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Teorema_de_desargues.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Teorema_de_desargues.svg/220px-Teorema_de_desargues.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Teorema_de_desargues.svg/330px-Teorema_de_desargues.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Teorema_de_desargues.svg/440px-Teorema_de_desargues.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Teorema_de_desargues.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Desargues%27_theorem" title="Desargues' theorem">Desargues' theorem</a>, a result in <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_geometry" title="Euclidean geometry">Euclidean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Projective_geometry" title="Projective geometry">projective geometry</a></div></div></div>
<p><b>Geometry</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <i> </i><span lang="el"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B3%CE%B5%CF%89%CE%BC%CE%B5%CF%84%CF%81%CE%AF%CE%B1#Greek" title="wikt:γεωμετρία">γεωμετρία</a></span><i> (<span lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i>geōmetría</i></span>)</i> 'land measurement'; from <i> </i><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B3%E1%BF%86#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:γῆ">γῆ</a></span><i> (<span lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i>gê</i></span>)</i> 'earth, land', and <i> </i><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%BC%CE%AD%CF%84%CF%81%CE%BF%CE%BD#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:μέτρον">μέτρον</a></span><i> (<span lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i>métron</i></span>)</i> 'a measure') is, with <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic" title="Arithmetic">arithmetic</a>, one of the oldest branches of <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>. It is concerned with properties of space such as the distance, shape, size, and relative position of figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Risi2015_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Risi2015-1">[1]</a></sup> A mathematician who works in the field of geometry is called a <i><a href="/wiki/List_of_geometers" title="List of geometers">geometer</a></i>.
</p><p>Until the 19th century, geometry was almost exclusively devoted to <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_geometry" title="Euclidean geometry">Euclidean geometry</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[a]</a></sup> which includes the notions of <a href="/wiki/Point_(geometry)" title="Point (geometry)">point</a>, <a href="/wiki/Line_(geometry)" title="Line (geometry)">line</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plane_(geometry)" title="Plane (geometry)">plane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distance" title="Distance">distance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angle" title="Angle">angle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surface_(mathematics)" title="Surface (mathematics)">surface</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Curve" title="Curve">curve</a>, as fundamental concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tabak_2014_xiv_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tabak_2014_xiv-3">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 19th century several discoveries enlarged dramatically the scope of geometry. One of the oldest such discoveries is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gauss" title="Gauss">Gauss</a>' <a href="/wiki/Theorema_Egregium" title="Theorema Egregium">Theorema Egregium</a> ("remarkable theorem") that asserts roughly that the <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_curvature" title="Gaussian curvature">Gaussian curvature</a> of a surface is independent from any specific <a href="/wiki/Embedding" title="Embedding">embedding</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_space" title="Euclidean space">Euclidean space</a>. This implies that surfaces can be studied <i>intrinsically</i>, that is, as stand-alone spaces, and has been expanded into the theory of <a href="/wiki/Manifold" title="Manifold">manifolds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Riemannian_geometry" title="Riemannian geometry">Riemannian geometry</a>.
</p><p>Later in the 19th century, it appeared that geometries without the <a href="/wiki/Parallel_postulate" title="Parallel postulate">parallel postulate</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-Euclidean_geometries" title="Non-Euclidean geometries">non-Euclidean geometries</a>) can be developed without introducing any contradiction. The geometry that underlies <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> is a famous application of non-Euclidean geometry.
</p><p>Since then, the scope of geometry has been greatly expanded, and the field has been split in many subfields that depend on the underlying methods—<a href="/wiki/Differential_geometry" title="Differential geometry">differential geometry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_geometry" title="Algebraic geometry">algebraic geometry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computational_geometry" title="Computational geometry">computational geometry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_topology" title="Algebraic topology">algebraic topology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Discrete_geometry" title="Discrete geometry">discrete geometry</a> (also known as <i>combinatorial geometry</i>), etc.—or on the properties of Euclidean spaces that are disregarded—<a href="/wiki/Projective_geometry" title="Projective geometry">projective geometry</a> that consider only alignment of points but not distance and parallelism, <a href="/wiki/Affine_geometry" title="Affine geometry">affine geometry</a> that omits the concept of angle and distance, <a href="/wiki/Finite_geometry" title="Finite geometry">finite geometry</a> that omits <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continuity_(mathematics)" title="Continuity (mathematics)">continuity</a>, and others.
</p><p>Originally developed to model the physical world, geometry has applications in almost all <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">sciences</a>, and also in <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a>, and other activities that are related to <a href="/wiki/Graphics" title="Graphics">graphics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meyer2006_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meyer2006-4">[3]</a></sup> Geometry also has applications in areas of mathematics that are apparently unrelated. For example, methods of algebraic geometry are fundamental in <a href="/wiki/Wiles%27s_proof_of_Fermat%27s_Last_Theorem" title="Wiles's proof of Fermat's Last Theorem">Wiles's proof</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fermat%27s_Last_Theorem" title="Fermat's Last Theorem">Fermat's Last Theorem</a>, a problem that was stated in terms of <a href="/wiki/Elementary_arithmetic" title="Elementary arithmetic">elementary arithmetic</a>, and remained unsolved for several centuries.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_geometry" title="History of geometry">History of geometry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Westerner_and_Arab_practicing_geometry_15th_century_manuscript.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2083" data-file-width="2130" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Westerner_and_Arab_practicing_geometry_15th_century_manuscript.jpg/220px-Westerner_and_Arab_practicing_geometry_15th_century_manuscript.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Westerner_and_Arab_practicing_geometry_15th_century_manuscript.jpg/330px-Westerner_and_Arab_practicing_geometry_15th_century_manuscript.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Westerner_and_Arab_practicing_geometry_15th_century_manuscript.jpg/440px-Westerner_and_Arab_practicing_geometry_15th_century_manuscript.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Westerner_and_Arab_practicing_geometry_15th_century_manuscript.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Europe" title="Ethnic groups in Europe">European</a> and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arab</a> practicing geometry in the 15th century</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest recorded beginnings of geometry can be traced to ancient <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a> in the 2nd millennium BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> Early geometry was a collection of empirically discovered principles concerning lengths, angles, areas, and volumes, which were developed to meet some practical need in <a href="/wiki/Surveying" title="Surveying">surveying</a>, <a href="/wiki/Construction" title="Construction">construction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, and various crafts. The earliest known texts on geometry are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_mathematics" title="Egyptian mathematics">Egyptian</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Rhind_Mathematical_Papyrus" title="Rhind Mathematical Papyrus">Rhind Papyrus</a></i> (2000–1800 BC) and <i><a href="/wiki/Moscow_Mathematical_Papyrus" title="Moscow Mathematical Papyrus">Moscow Papyrus</a></i> (c. 1890 BC), and the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_mathematics" title="Babylonian mathematics">Babylonian clay tablets</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Plimpton_322" title="Plimpton 322">Plimpton 322</a> (1900 BC). For example, the Moscow Papyrus gives a formula for calculating the volume of a truncated pyramid, or <a href="/wiki/Frustum" title="Frustum">frustum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyer_1991_loc=Egypt_p._19_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boyer_1991_loc=Egypt_p._19-7">[6]</a></sup> Later clay tablets (350–50 BC) demonstrate that Babylonian astronomers implemented <a href="/wiki/Trapezoid" title="Trapezoid">trapezoid</a> procedures for computing Jupiter's position and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Displacement_(vector)" title="Displacement (vector)">motion</a> within time-velocity space. These geometric procedures anticipated the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Calculators" title="Oxford Calculators">Oxford Calculators</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Mean_speed_theorem" title="Mean speed theorem">mean speed theorem</a>, by 14 centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup> South of Egypt the <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">ancient Nubians</a> established a system of geometry including early versions of sun clocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 7th century BC, the <a href="/wiki/Greek_mathematics" title="Greek mathematics">Greek</a> mathematician <a href="/wiki/Thales_of_Miletus" title="Thales of Miletus">Thales of Miletus</a> used geometry to solve problems such as calculating the height of pyramids and the distance of ships from the shore.  He is credited with the first use of deductive reasoning applied to geometry, by deriving four corollaries to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales%27_theorem" title="Thales' theorem">Thales' theorem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyer_1991_loc=Ionia_and_the_Pythagoreans_p._43_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boyer_1991_loc=Ionia_and_the_Pythagoreans_p._43-11">[10]</a></sup> Pythagoras established the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pythagoreans" title="Pythagoreans">Pythagorean School</a>, which is credited with the first proof of the <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_theorem" title="Pythagorean theorem">Pythagorean theorem</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup>  though the statement of the theorem has a long history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eudoxus_of_Cnidus" title="Eudoxus of Cnidus">Eudoxus</a> (408–c. 355 BC) developed the <a href="/wiki/Method_of_exhaustion" title="Method of exhaustion">method of exhaustion</a>, which allowed the calculation of areas and volumes of curvilinear figures,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> as well as a theory of ratios that avoided the problem of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incommensurable_magnitudes" title="Incommensurable magnitudes">incommensurable magnitudes</a>, which enabled subsequent geometers to make significant advances. Around 300 BC, geometry was revolutionized by Euclid, whose <i><a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid's Elements">Elements</a></i>, widely considered the most successful and influential textbook of all time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> introduced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematical_rigor" title="Mathematical rigor">mathematical rigor</a> through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axiomatic_method" title="Axiomatic method">axiomatic method</a> and is the earliest example of the format still used in mathematics today, that of definition, axiom, theorem, and proof. Although most of the contents of the <i>Elements</i> were already known, Euclid arranged them into a single, coherent logical framework.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyer_1991_loc=Euclid_of_Alexandria_p._104_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boyer_1991_loc=Euclid_of_Alexandria_p._104-17">[16]</a></sup> The <i>Elements</i> was known to all educated people in the West until the middle of the 20th century and its contents are still taught in geometry classes today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> (c. 287–212 BC) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Italy" title="Syracuse, Italy">Syracuse</a> used the <a href="/wiki/Method_of_exhaustion" title="Method of exhaustion">method of exhaustion</a> to calculate the <a href="/wiki/Area" title="Area">area</a> under the arc of a <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabola</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Series_(mathematics)" title="Series (mathematics)">summation of an infinite series</a>, and gave remarkably accurate approximations of <a href="/wiki/Pi" title="Pi">pi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> He also studied the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archimedes_spiral" title="Archimedes spiral">spiral</a> bearing his name and obtained formulas for the <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volumes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Surface_of_revolution" title="Surface of revolution">surfaces of revolution</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Woman_teaching_geometry.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1148" data-file-width="1039" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Woman_teaching_geometry.jpg/190px-Woman_teaching_geometry.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Woman_teaching_geometry.jpg/285px-Woman_teaching_geometry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Woman_teaching_geometry.jpg/380px-Woman_teaching_geometry.jpg 2x" width="190"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Woman_teaching_geometry.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Woman teaching geometry</i>. Illustration at the beginning of a medieval translation of <a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid's Elements">Euclid's Elements</a>, (c. 1310).</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Indian_mathematics" title="Indian mathematics">Indian</a> mathematicians also made many important contributions in geometry. The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satapatha_Brahmana" title="Satapatha Brahmana">Satapatha Brahmana</a></i> (3rd century BC) contains rules for ritual geometric constructions that are similar to the <i><a href="/wiki/Shulba_Sutras" title="Shulba Sutras">Sulba Sutras</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Staal_1999_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Staal_1999-20">[19]</a></sup> According to (<a href="#CITEREFHayashi2005">Hayashi 2005</a>, p. 363), the <i>Śulba Sūtras</i> contain "the earliest extant verbal expression of the Pythagorean Theorem in the world, although it had already been known to the Old Babylonians. They contain lists of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pythagorean_triples" title="Pythagorean triples">Pythagorean triples</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> which are particular cases of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diophantine_equations" title="Diophantine equations">Diophantine equations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cooke198_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-cooke198-22">[21]</a></sup>
In the <a href="/wiki/Bakhshali_manuscript" title="Bakhshali manuscript">Bakhshali manuscript</a>, there is a handful of geometric problems (including problems about volumes of irregular solids). The Bakhshali manuscript also "employs a decimal place value system with a dot for zero."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hayashi2005-371_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-hayashi2005-371-23">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Aryabhatiya" title="Aryabhatiya">Aryabhatiya</a></i> (499) includes the computation of areas and volumes.
<a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta" title="Brahmagupta">Brahmagupta</a> wrote his astronomical work <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmasphutasiddhanta" title="Brahmasphutasiddhanta"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Brāhma Sphuṭa Siddhānta</i></span></a></i> in 628. Chapter 12, containing 66 <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> verses, was divided into two sections: "basic operations" (including cube roots, fractions, ratio and proportion, and barter) and "practical mathematics" (including mixture, mathematical series, plane figures, stacking bricks, sawing of timber, and piling of grain).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hayashi2003-p121-122_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-hayashi2003-p121-122-24">[23]</a></sup> In the latter section, he stated his famous theorem on the diagonals of a <a href="/wiki/Cyclic_quadrilateral" title="Cyclic quadrilateral">cyclic quadrilateral</a>. Chapter 12 also included a formula for the area of a cyclic quadrilateral (a generalization of <a href="/wiki/Heron%27s_formula" title="Heron's formula">Heron's formula</a>), as well as a complete description of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rational_triangle" title="Rational triangle">rational triangles</a> (<i>i.e.</i> triangles with rational sides and rational areas).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hayashi2003-p121-122_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-hayashi2003-p121-122-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematics_in_medieval_Islam" title="Mathematics in medieval Islam">mathematics in medieval Islam</a> contributed to the development of geometry, especially <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_geometry" title="Algebraic geometry">algebraic geometry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al-Mahani" title="Al-Mahani">Al-Mahani</a> (b. 853) conceived the idea of reducing geometrical problems such as duplicating the cube to problems in algebra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Th%C4%81bit_ibn_Qurra" title="Thābit ibn Qurra">Thābit ibn Qurra</a> (known as Thebit in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>) (836–901) dealt with <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic" title="Arithmetic">arithmetic</a> operations applied to <a href="/wiki/Ratio" title="Ratio">ratios</a> of geometrical quantities, and contributed to the development of <a href="/wiki/Analytic_geometry" title="Analytic geometry">analytic geometry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-28">[27]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omar_Khayy%C3%A1m" title="Omar Khayyám">Omar Khayyám</a> (1048–1131) found geometric solutions to <a href="/wiki/Cubic_equation" title="Cubic equation">cubic equations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup>  The theorems of <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Ibn al-Haytham</a> (Alhazen), Omar Khayyam and <a href="/wiki/Nasir_al-Din_al-Tusi" title="Nasir al-Din al-Tusi">Nasir al-Din al-Tusi</a> on <a href="/wiki/Quadrilateral" title="Quadrilateral">quadrilaterals</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Lambert_quadrilateral" title="Lambert quadrilateral">Lambert quadrilateral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saccheri_quadrilateral" title="Saccheri quadrilateral">Saccheri quadrilateral</a>, were early results in <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_geometry" title="Hyperbolic geometry">hyperbolic geometry</a>, and along with their alternative postulates, such as <a href="/wiki/Playfair%27s_axiom" title="Playfair's axiom">Playfair's axiom</a>, these works had a considerable influence on the development of non-Euclidean geometry among later European geometers, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Witelo" title="Witelo">Witelo</a> (c. 1230–c. 1314), <a href="/wiki/Gersonides" title="Gersonides">Gersonides</a> (1288–1344), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfonso" title="Alfonso">Alfonso</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Wallis" title="John Wallis">John Wallis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Girolamo_Saccheri" title="Giovanni Girolamo Saccheri">Giovanni Girolamo Saccheri</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="this contradicts the assertion (below) that non-Euclidean geometry was discovered only in the 19th century (September 2020)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:Geometry#Dubious" title="Talk:Geometry">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 17th century, there were two important developments in geometry. The first was the creation of analytic geometry, or geometry with <a href="/wiki/Coordinate_system" title="Coordinate system">coordinates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Equation" title="Equation">equations</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a> (1596–1650) and <a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_Fermat" title="Pierre de Fermat">Pierre de Fermat</a> (1601–1665).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyer2012_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boyer2012-31">[30]</a></sup> This was a necessary precursor to the development of <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a> and a precise quantitative science of <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards2012_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards2012-32">[31]</a></sup> The second geometric development of this period was the systematic study of <a href="/wiki/Projective_geometry" title="Projective geometry">projective geometry</a> by <a href="/wiki/Girard_Desargues" title="Girard Desargues">Girard Desargues</a> (1591–1661).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FieldGray2012_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FieldGray2012-33">[32]</a></sup> Projective geometry studies properties of shapes which are unchanged under <a href="/wiki/Projection_(linear_algebra)" title="Projection (linear algebra)">projections</a> and <a href="/wiki/Section_(fiber_bundle)" title="Section (fiber bundle)">sections</a>, especially as they relate to <a href="/wiki/Perspective_(graphical)" title="Perspective (graphical)">artistic perspective</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wylie2011_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wylie2011-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two developments in geometry in the 19th century changed the way it had been studied previously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gray2011_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gray2011-35">[34]</a></sup> These were the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Non-Euclidean_geometry" title="Non-Euclidean geometry">non-Euclidean geometries</a> by Nikolai Ivanovich Lobachevsky, János Bolyai and Carl Friedrich Gauss and of the formulation of <a href="/wiki/Symmetry" title="Symmetry">symmetry</a> as the central consideration in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erlangen_Programme" title="Erlangen Programme">Erlangen Programme</a> of <a href="/wiki/Felix_Klein" title="Felix Klein">Felix Klein</a> (which generalized the Euclidean and non-Euclidean geometries). Two of the master geometers of the time were <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Riemann" title="Bernhard Riemann">Bernhard Riemann</a> (1826–1866), working primarily with tools from <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_analysis" title="Mathematical analysis">mathematical analysis</a>, and introducing the <a href="/wiki/Riemann_surface" title="Riemann surface">Riemann surface</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Henri_Poincar%C3%A9" title="Henri Poincaré">Henri Poincaré</a>, the founder of <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_topology" title="Algebraic topology">algebraic topology</a> and the geometric theory of <a href="/wiki/Dynamical_system" title="Dynamical system">dynamical systems</a>. As a consequence of these major changes in the conception of geometry, the concept of "space" became something rich and varied, and the natural background for theories as different as <a href="/wiki/Complex_analysis" title="Complex analysis">complex analysis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bayro-Corrochano2018_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bayro-Corrochano2018-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_concepts">Main concepts</span></h2>
<p>The following are some of the most important concepts in geometry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tabak_2014_xiv_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tabak_2014_xiv-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt,_W._2002_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt,_W._2002-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kline1990_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kline1990-38">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Axioms">Axioms</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parallel_postulate_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Parallel_postulate_en.svg/220px-Parallel_postulate_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Parallel_postulate_en.svg/330px-Parallel_postulate_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Parallel_postulate_en.svg/440px-Parallel_postulate_en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parallel_postulate_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration of Euclid's <a href="/wiki/Parallel_postulate" title="Parallel postulate">parallel postulate</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_geometry" title="Euclidean geometry">Euclidean geometry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">Axiom</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> took an abstract approach to geometry in his <a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid's Elements">Elements</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Katz2000_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Katz2000-39">[38]</a></sup> one of the most influential books ever written.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berlinski2014_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berlinski2014-40">[39]</a></sup> Euclid introduced certain <a href="/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axioms</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postulate" title="Postulate">postulates</a>, expressing primary or self-evident properties of points, lines, and planes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartshorne2013_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hartshorne2013-41">[40]</a></sup> He proceeded to rigorously deduce other properties by mathematical reasoning. The characteristic feature of Euclid's approach to geometry was its rigor, and it has come to be known as <i>axiomatic</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Synthetic_geometry" title="Synthetic geometry">synthetic</a></i> geometry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HerbstFujita2017_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-HerbstFujita2017-42">[41]</a></sup> At the start of the 19th century, the discovery of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-Euclidean_geometries" title="Non-Euclidean geometries">non-Euclidean geometries</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikolai_Ivanovich_Lobachevsky" title="Nikolai Ivanovich Lobachevsky">Nikolai Ivanovich Lobachevsky</a> (1792–1856), <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1nos_Bolyai" title="János Bolyai">János Bolyai</a> (1802–1860), <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a> (1777–1855) and others<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yaglom2012_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yaglom2012-43">[42]</a></sup> led to a revival of interest in this discipline, and in the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/David_Hilbert" title="David Hilbert">David Hilbert</a> (1862–1943) employed axiomatic reasoning in an attempt to provide a modern foundation of geometry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holme2010_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holme2010-44">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Objects">Objects</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Points">Points</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Point_(geometry)" title="Point (geometry)">Point (geometry)</a></div>
<p>Points are generally considered fundamental objects for building geometry. They may be defined by the properties that they must have, as in Euclid's definition as "that which has no part",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EuclidAll_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-EuclidAll-45">[44]</a></sup> or in <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_geometry" title="Synthetic geometry">synthetic geometry</a>. In modern mathematics, they are generally defined as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Element_(set_theory)" title="Element (set theory)">elements</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Set_(mathematics)" title="Set (mathematics)">set</a> called <a href="/wiki/Space_(mathematics)" title="Space (mathematics)">space</a>, which is itself <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axiomatically" title="Axiomatically">axiomatically</a> defined.
</p><p>With these modern definitions, every geometric shape is defined as a set of points; this is not the case in synthetic geometry, where a line is another fundamental object that is not viewed as the set of the points through which it passes.
</p><p>However, there are modern geometries in which points are not primitive objects, or even without points.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> One of the oldest such geometries is <a href="/wiki/Whitehead%27s_point-free_geometry" title="Whitehead's point-free geometry">Whitehead's point-free geometry</a>, formulated by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">Alfred North Whitehead</a> in 1919–1920.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lines">Lines</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Line_(geometry)" title="Line (geometry)">Line (geometry)</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> described a line as "breadthless length" which "lies equally with respect to the points on itself".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EuclidAll_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-EuclidAll-45">[44]</a></sup> In modern mathematics, given the multitude of geometries, the concept of a line is closely tied to the way the geometry is described. For instance, in <a href="/wiki/Analytic_geometry" title="Analytic geometry">analytic geometry</a>, a line in the plane is often defined as the set of points whose coordinates satisfy a given <a href="/wiki/Linear_equation" title="Linear equation">linear equation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> but in a more abstract setting, such as <a href="/wiki/Incidence_geometry" title="Incidence geometry">incidence geometry</a>, a line may be an independent object, distinct from the set of points which lie on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> In differential geometry, a <a href="/wiki/Geodesic" title="Geodesic">geodesic</a> is a generalization of the notion of a line to <a href="/wiki/Manifold" title="Manifold">curved spaces</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Planes">Planes</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plane_(geometry)" title="Plane (geometry)">Plane (geometry)</a></div>
<p>In Euclidean geometry a <a href="/wiki/Plane_(geometry)" title="Plane (geometry)">plane</a> is a flat, two-dimensional surface that extends infinitely;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EuclidAll_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-EuclidAll-45">[44]</a></sup> the definitions for other types of geometries are generalizations of that. Planes are used in many areas of geometry. For instance, planes can be studied as a <a href="/wiki/Surface_(topology)" title="Surface (topology)">topological surface</a> without reference to distances or angles;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Munkres_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Munkres-51">[50]</a></sup> it can be studied as an <a href="/wiki/Affine_space" title="Affine space">affine space</a>, where collinearity and ratios can be studied but not distances;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> it can be studied as the <a href="/wiki/Complex_plane" title="Complex plane">complex plane</a> using techniques of <a href="/wiki/Complex_analysis" title="Complex analysis">complex analysis</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> and so on.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Angles">Angles</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Angle" title="Angle">Angle</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> defines a plane <a href="/wiki/Angle" title="Angle">angle</a> as the inclination to each other, in a plane, of two lines which meet each other, and do not lie straight with respect to each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EuclidAll_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-EuclidAll-45">[44]</a></sup> In modern terms, an angle is the figure formed by two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ray_(geometry)" title="Ray (geometry)">rays</a>, called the <i>sides</i> of the angle, sharing a common endpoint, called the <i><a href="/wiki/Vertex_(geometry)" title="Vertex (geometry)">vertex</a></i> of the angle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Angle_obtuse_acute_straight.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="445" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Angle_obtuse_acute_straight.svg/220px-Angle_obtuse_acute_straight.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Angle_obtuse_acute_straight.svg/330px-Angle_obtuse_acute_straight.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Angle_obtuse_acute_straight.svg/440px-Angle_obtuse_acute_straight.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Angle_obtuse_acute_straight.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Acute (a), obtuse (b), and straight (c) angles. The acute and obtuse angles are also known as oblique angles.</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_geometry" title="Euclidean geometry">Euclidean geometry</a>, angles are used to study <a href="/wiki/Polygon" title="Polygon">polygons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Triangle" title="Triangle">triangles</a>, as well as forming an object of study in their own right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EuclidAll_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-EuclidAll-45">[44]</a></sup> The study of the angles of a triangle or of angles in a <a href="/wiki/Unit_circle" title="Unit circle">unit circle</a> forms the basis of <a href="/wiki/Trigonometry" title="Trigonometry">trigonometry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Differential_geometry" title="Differential geometry">differential geometry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a>, the angles between <a href="/wiki/Plane_curve" title="Plane curve">plane curves</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_curve" title="Space curve">space curves</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surface_(geometry)" title="Surface (geometry)">surfaces</a> can be calculated using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Derivative_(calculus)" title="Derivative (calculus)">derivative</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stewart_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stewart-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Curves">Curves</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curve_(geometry)" title="Curve (geometry)">Curve (geometry)</a></div>
<p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curve_(geometry)" title="Curve (geometry)">curve</a> is a 1-dimensional object that may be straight (like a line) or not; curves in 2-dimensional space are called <a href="/wiki/Plane_curve" title="Plane curve">plane curves</a> and those in 3-dimensional space are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_curve" title="Space curve">space curves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>In topology, a curve is defined by a function from an interval of the real numbers to another space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Munkres_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Munkres-51">[50]</a></sup> In differential geometry, the same definition is used, but the defining function is required to be differentiable <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carmo_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carmo-59">[58]</a></sup> Algebraic geometry studies <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_curve" title="Algebraic curve">algebraic curves</a>, which are defined as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algebraic_varieties" title="Algebraic varieties">algebraic varieties</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dimension_of_an_algebraic_variety" title="Dimension of an algebraic variety">dimension</a> one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mumford_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-mumford-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Surfaces">Surfaces</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Surface_(mathematics)" title="Surface (mathematics)">Surface (mathematics)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sphere_wireframe.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Sphere_wireframe.svg/190px-Sphere_wireframe.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Sphere_wireframe.svg/285px-Sphere_wireframe.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Sphere_wireframe.svg/380px-Sphere_wireframe.svg.png 2x" width="190"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sphere_wireframe.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sphere is a surface that can be defined parametrically (by <span class="nowrap"><i>x</i> = <i>r</i> sin <i>θ</i> cos <i>φ</i>,</span> <span class="nowrap"><i>y</i> = <i>r</i> sin <i>θ</i> sin <i>φ</i>,</span> <span class="nowrap"><i>z</i> = <i>r</i> cos <i>θ</i>)</span> or implicitly (by <span class="nowrap"><i>x</i><sup>2</sup> + <i>y</i><sup>2</sup> + <i>z</i><sup>2</sup> − <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> = 0</span>.)</div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Surface_(mathematics)" title="Surface (mathematics)">surface</a> is a two-dimensional object, such as a sphere or paraboloid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Differential_geometry" title="Differential geometry">differential geometry</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carmo_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carmo-59">[58]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Topology" title="Topology">topology</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Munkres_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Munkres-51">[50]</a></sup> surfaces are described by two-dimensional 'patches' (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neighborhood_(topology)" title="Neighborhood (topology)">neighborhoods</a>) that are assembled by <a href="/wiki/Diffeomorphism" title="Diffeomorphism">diffeomorphisms</a> or <a href="/wiki/Homeomorphism" title="Homeomorphism">homeomorphisms</a>, respectively. In algebraic geometry, surfaces are described by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polynomial_equation" title="Polynomial equation">polynomial equations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mumford_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-mumford-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Manifolds">Manifolds</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Manifold" title="Manifold">Manifold</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Manifold" title="Manifold">manifold</a> is a generalization of the concepts of curve and surface. In <a href="/wiki/Topology" title="Topology">topology</a>, a manifold is a <a href="/wiki/Topological_space" title="Topological space">topological space</a> where every point has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neighborhood_(topology)" title="Neighborhood (topology)">neighborhood</a> that is <a href="/wiki/Homeomorphism" title="Homeomorphism">homeomorphic</a> to Euclidean space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Munkres_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-Munkres-51">[50]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Differential_geometry" title="Differential geometry">differential geometry</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Differentiable_manifold" title="Differentiable manifold">differentiable manifold</a> is a space where each neighborhood is <a href="/wiki/Diffeomorphism" title="Diffeomorphism">diffeomorphic</a> to Euclidean space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carmo_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-Carmo-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Manifolds are used extensively in physics, including in <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> and <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Length.2C_area.2C_and_volume"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Length,_area,_and_volume">Length, area, and volume</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Length" title="Length">Length</a>, <a href="/wiki/Area" title="Area">Area</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">Volume</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Area#List_of_formulas" title="Area">Area § List of formulas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Volume#Volume_formulas" title="Volume">Volume § Volume formulas</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Length" title="Length">Length</a>, <a href="/wiki/Area" title="Area">area</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a> describe the size or extent of an object in one dimension, two dimension, and three dimensions respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Treese2018_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Treese2018-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_geometry" title="Euclidean geometry">Euclidean geometry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Analytic_geometry" title="Analytic geometry">analytic geometry</a>, the length of a line segment can often be calculated by the <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_theorem" title="Pythagorean theorem">Pythagorean theorem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cannon2017_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cannon2017-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Area and volume can be defined as fundamental quantities separate from length, or they can be described and calculated in terms of lengths in a plane or 3-dimensional space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Treese2018_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Treese2018-63">[62]</a></sup> Mathematicians have found many explicit <a href="/wiki/Area#List_of_formulas" title="Area">formulas for area</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volume#Formulas" title="Volume">formulas for volume</a> of various geometric objects. In <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a>, area and volume can be defined in terms of <a href="/wiki/Integral" title="Integral">integrals</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Riemann_integral" title="Riemann integral">Riemann integral</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strang1991_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strang1991-65">[64]</a></sup> or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lebesgue_integral" title="Lebesgue integral">Lebesgue integral</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bear2002_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bear2002-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Metrics_and_measures">Metrics and measures</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_(mathematics)" title="Metric (mathematics)">Metric (mathematics)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Measure_(mathematics)" title="Measure (mathematics)">Measure (mathematics)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_pythagoras.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="475" data-file-width="871" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Chinese_pythagoras.jpg/220px-Chinese_pythagoras.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Chinese_pythagoras.jpg/330px-Chinese_pythagoras.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Chinese_pythagoras.jpg/440px-Chinese_pythagoras.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_pythagoras.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Visual checking of the <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_theorem" title="Pythagorean theorem">Pythagorean theorem</a> for the (3, 4, 5) <a href="/wiki/Triangle" title="Triangle">triangle</a> as in the <a href="/wiki/Zhoubi_Suanjing" title="Zhoubi Suanjing">Zhoubi Suanjing</a> 500–200 BC. The Pythagorean theorem is a consequence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euclidean_metric" title="Euclidean metric">Euclidean metric</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The concept of length or distance can be generalized, leading to the idea of <a href="/wiki/Metric_space" title="Metric space">metrics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> For instance, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euclidean_metric" title="Euclidean metric">Euclidean metric</a> measures the distance between points in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euclidean_plane" title="Euclidean plane">Euclidean plane</a>, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_metric" title="Hyperbolic metric">hyperbolic metric</a> measures the distance in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_plane" title="Hyperbolic plane">hyperbolic plane</a>. Other important examples of metrics include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorentz_metric" title="Lorentz metric">Lorentz metric</a> of <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a> and the semi-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riemannian_metric" title="Riemannian metric">Riemannian metrics</a> of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a different direction, the concepts of length, area and volume are extended by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Measure_theory" title="Measure theory">measure theory</a>, which studies methods of assigning a size or <i>measure</i> to <a href="/wiki/Set_(mathematics)" title="Set (mathematics)">sets</a>, where the measures follow rules similar to those of classical area and volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tao2011_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tao2011-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Congruence_and_similarity">Congruence and similarity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Congruence_(geometry)" title="Congruence (geometry)">Congruence (geometry)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Similarity_(geometry)" title="Similarity (geometry)">Similarity (geometry)</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Congruence_(geometry)" title="Congruence (geometry)">Congruence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Similarity_(geometry)" title="Similarity (geometry)">similarity</a> are concepts that describe when two shapes have similar characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Libeskind2008_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Libeskind2008-70">[69]</a></sup> In Euclidean geometry, similarity is used to describe objects that have the same shape, while congruence is used to describe objects that are the same in both size and shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freitag2013_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freitag2013-71">[70]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hilbert" title="Hilbert">Hilbert</a>, in his work on creating a more rigorous foundation for geometry, treated congruence as an undefined term whose properties are defined by <a href="/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axioms</a>.
</p><p>Congruence and similarity are generalized in <a href="/wiki/Transformation_geometry" title="Transformation geometry">transformation geometry</a>, which studies the properties of geometric objects that are preserved by different kinds of transformations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin2012_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martin2012-72">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compass_and_straightedge_constructions">Compass and straightedge constructions</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compass_and_straightedge_constructions" title="Compass and straightedge constructions">Compass and straightedge constructions</a></div>
<p>Classical geometers paid special attention to constructing geometric objects that had been described in some other way. Classically, the only instruments used in most geometric constructions are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compass_(drafting)" title="Compass (drafting)">compass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruler" title="Ruler">straightedge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[b]</a></sup> Also, every construction had to be complete in a finite number of steps. However, some problems turned out to be difficult or impossible to solve by these means alone, and ingenious constructions using <a href="/wiki/Neusis_construction" title="Neusis construction">neusis</a>, parabolas and other curves, or mechanical devices, were found.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dimension">Dimension</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dimension" title="Dimension">Dimension</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Von_Koch_curve.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="312" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Von_Koch_curve.gif/220px-Von_Koch_curve.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Von_Koch_curve.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Von_Koch_curve.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Koch_snowflake" title="Koch snowflake">Koch snowflake</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Fractal_dimension" title="Fractal dimension">fractal dimension</a>=log4/log3 and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Topological_dimension" title="Topological dimension">topological dimension</a>=1</div></div></div>
<p>Where the traditional geometry allowed dimensions 1 (a <a href="/wiki/Line_(geometry)" title="Line (geometry)">line</a>), 2 (a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plane_(mathematics)" title="Plane (mathematics)">plane</a>) and 3 (our ambient world conceived of as <a href="/wiki/Three-dimensional_space" title="Three-dimensional space">three-dimensional space</a>), mathematicians and physicists have used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Higher_dimension" title="Higher dimension">higher dimensions</a> for nearly two centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blacklock2018_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blacklock2018-74">[72]</a></sup> One example of a mathematical use for higher dimensions is the <a href="/wiki/Configuration_space_(physics)" title="Configuration space (physics)">configuration space</a> of a physical system, which has a dimension equal to the system's <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom" title="Degrees of freedom">degrees of freedom</a>. For instance, the configuration of a screw can be described by five coordinates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joly1895_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Joly1895-75">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/General_topology" title="General topology">general topology</a>, the concept of dimension has been extended from <a href="/wiki/Natural_number" title="Natural number">natural numbers</a>, to infinite dimension (<a href="/wiki/Hilbert_space" title="Hilbert space">Hilbert spaces</a>, for example) and  positive <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real numbers</a> (in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fractal_geometry" title="Fractal geometry">fractal geometry</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Temam2013_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Temam2013-76">[74]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_geometry" title="Algebraic geometry">algebraic geometry</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dimension_of_an_algebraic_variety" title="Dimension of an algebraic variety">dimension of an algebraic variety</a> has received a number of apparently different definitions, which are all equivalent in the most common cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JacobLam1994_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-JacobLam1994-77">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symmetry">Symmetry</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Symmetry" title="Symmetry">Symmetry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Order-3_heptakis_heptagonal_tiling.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Order-3_heptakis_heptagonal_tiling.png/220px-Order-3_heptakis_heptagonal_tiling.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Order-3_heptakis_heptagonal_tiling.png/330px-Order-3_heptakis_heptagonal_tiling.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Order-3_heptakis_heptagonal_tiling.png/440px-Order-3_heptakis_heptagonal_tiling.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Order-3_heptakis_heptagonal_tiling.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Order-3_bisected_heptagonal_tiling" title="Order-3 bisected heptagonal tiling">tiling</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_geometry" title="Hyperbolic geometry">hyperbolic plane</a></div></div></div>
<p>The theme of <a href="/wiki/Symmetry" title="Symmetry">symmetry</a> in geometry is nearly as old as the science of geometry itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stewart2008_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stewart2008-78">[76]</a></sup> Symmetric shapes such as the <a href="/wiki/Circle" title="Circle">circle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Regular_polygon" title="Regular polygon">regular polygons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Platonic_solid" title="Platonic solid">platonic solids</a> held deep significance for many ancient philosophers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexey2009_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alexey2009-79">[77]</a></sup> and were investigated in detail before the time of Euclid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartshorne2013_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hartshorne2013-41">[40]</a></sup> Symmetric patterns occur in nature and were artistically rendered in a multitude of forms, including the graphics of <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>, <a href="/wiki/M._C._Escher" title="M. C. Escher">M. C. Escher</a>, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hahn1998_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hahn1998-80">[78]</a></sup> In the second half of the 19th century, the relationship between symmetry and geometry came under intense scrutiny. <a href="/wiki/Felix_Klein" title="Felix Klein">Felix Klein</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Erlangen_program" title="Erlangen program">Erlangen program</a> proclaimed that, in a very precise sense, symmetry, expressed via the notion of a transformation <a href="/wiki/Group_(mathematics)" title="Group (mathematics)">group</a>, determines what geometry <i>is</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cantwell2002_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cantwell2002-81">[79]</a></sup> Symmetry in classical <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_geometry" title="Euclidean geometry">Euclidean geometry</a> is represented by <a href="/wiki/Congruence_(geometry)" title="Congruence (geometry)">congruences</a> and rigid motions, whereas in <a href="/wiki/Projective_geometry" title="Projective geometry">projective geometry</a> an analogous role is played by <a href="/wiki/Collineation" title="Collineation">collineations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geometric_transformation" title="Geometric transformation">geometric transformations</a> that take straight lines into straight lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RosenfeldWiebe2013_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-RosenfeldWiebe2013-82">[80]</a></sup> However it was in the new geometries of Bolyai and Lobachevsky, Riemann, <a href="/wiki/William_Kingdon_Clifford" title="William Kingdon Clifford">Clifford</a> and Klein, and <a href="/wiki/Sophus_Lie" title="Sophus Lie">Sophus Lie</a> that Klein's idea to 'define a geometry via its <a href="/wiki/Symmetry_group" title="Symmetry group">symmetry group</a>' found its inspiration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pesic2007_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pesic2007-83">[81]</a></sup> Both discrete and continuous symmetries play prominent roles in geometry, the former in <a href="/wiki/Topology" title="Topology">topology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geometric_group_theory" title="Geometric group theory">geometric group theory</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaku2012_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaku2012-84">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BestvinaSageev2014_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-BestvinaSageev2014-85">[83]</a></sup> the latter in <a href="/wiki/Lie_theory" title="Lie theory">Lie theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Riemannian_geometry" title="Riemannian geometry">Riemannian geometry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steeb1996_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steeb1996-86">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Misner2005_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Misner2005-87">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>A different type of symmetry is the principle of <a href="/wiki/Duality_(projective_geometry)" title="Duality (projective geometry)">duality</a>  in <a href="/wiki/Projective_geometry" title="Projective geometry">projective geometry</a>, among other fields. This meta-phenomenon can roughly be described as follows: in any <a href="/wiki/Theorem" title="Theorem">theorem</a>, exchange <i>point</i> with <i>plane</i>, <i>join</i> with <i>meet</i>, <i>lies in</i> with <i>contains</i>, and the result is an equally true theorem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dowling1917_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dowling1917-88">[86]</a></sup> A similar and closely related form of duality exists between a <a href="/wiki/Vector_space" title="Vector space">vector space</a> and its <a href="/wiki/Dual_space" title="Dual space">dual space</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gierz2006_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gierz2006-89">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_geometry">Contemporary geometry</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Euclidean_geometry">Euclidean geometry</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_geometry" title="Euclidean geometry">Euclidean geometry</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Euclidean_geometry" title="Euclidean geometry">Euclidean geometry</a> is geometry in its classical sense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ButtsBrown2012_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-ButtsBrown2012-90">[88]</a></sup> As it models the space of the physical world, it is used in many scientific areas, such as <a href="/wiki/Mechanics" title="Mechanics">mechanics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crystallography" title="Crystallography">crystallography</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup> and many technical fields, such as <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbot2013_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abbot2013-92">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HerseyHersey2001_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-HerseyHersey2001-93">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Geodesy" title="Geodesy">geodesy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanícekKrakiwsky2015_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-VanícekKrakiwsky2015-94">[92]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aerodynamics" title="Aerodynamics">aerodynamics</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CummingsMorton2015_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-CummingsMorton2015-95">[93]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Navigation" title="Navigation">navigation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams1998_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams1998-96">[94]</a></sup> The mandatory educational curriculum of the majority of nations includes the study of Euclidean concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Point_(geometry)" title="Point (geometry)">points</a>, <a href="/wiki/Line_(geometry)" title="Line (geometry)">lines</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plane_(mathematics)" title="Plane (mathematics)">planes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angle" title="Angle">angles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Triangle" title="Triangle">triangles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Congruence_(geometry)" title="Congruence (geometry)">congruence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Similarity_(geometry)" title="Similarity (geometry)">similarity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solid_figure" title="Solid figure">solid figures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Circle" title="Circle">circles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Analytic_geometry" title="Analytic geometry">analytic geometry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt,_W._2002_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt,_W._2002-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Differential_geometry">Differential geometry</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hyperbolic_triangle.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Hyperbolic_triangle.svg/220px-Hyperbolic_triangle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Hyperbolic_triangle.svg/330px-Hyperbolic_triangle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Hyperbolic_triangle.svg/440px-Hyperbolic_triangle.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hyperbolic_triangle.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Differential_geometry" title="Differential geometry">Differential geometry</a> uses tools from <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a> to study problems involving curvature.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Differential_geometry" title="Differential geometry">Differential geometry</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Differential_geometry" title="Differential geometry">Differential geometry</a> uses techniques of <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Linear_algebra" title="Linear algebra">linear algebra</a> to study problems in geometry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walschap2015_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walschap2015-97">[95]</a></sup> It has applications in <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flanders2012_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flanders2012-98">[96]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Econometrics" title="Econometrics">econometrics</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarriottSalmon2000_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-MarriottSalmon2000-99">[97]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bioinformatics" title="Bioinformatics">bioinformatics</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HePetoukhov2011_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-HePetoukhov2011-100">[98]</a></sup> among others.
</p><p>In particular, differential geometry is of importance to <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_physics" title="Mathematical physics">mathematical physics</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>'s <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> postulation that the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> is <a href="/wiki/Curvature" title="Curvature">curved</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dirac2016_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dirac2016-101">[99]</a></sup> Differential geometry can either be <i>intrinsic</i> (meaning that the spaces it considers are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smooth_manifold" title="Smooth manifold">smooth manifolds</a> whose geometric structure is governed by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riemannian_metric" title="Riemannian metric">Riemannian metric</a>, which determines how distances are measured near each point) or <i>extrinsic</i> (where the object under study is a part of some ambient flat Euclidean space).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AyJost2017_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-AyJost2017-102">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-Euclidean_geometry">Non-Euclidean geometry</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Non-Euclidean_geometry" title="Non-Euclidean geometry">Non-Euclidean geometry</a></div>
<p>Euclidean geometry was not the only historical form of geometry studied. <a href="/wiki/Spherical_geometry" title="Spherical geometry">Spherical geometry</a> has long been used by astronomers, astrologers, and navigators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosenfeld2012_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenfeld2012-103">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> argued that there is only one, <i>absolute</i>, geometry, which is known to be true <i>a priori</i> by an inner faculty of mind: Euclidean geometry was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthetic_a_priori" title="Synthetic a priori">synthetic a priori</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup>  This view was at first somewhat challenged by thinkers such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saccheri" title="Saccheri">Saccheri</a>, then finally overturned by the revolutionary discovery of <a href="/wiki/Non-Euclidean_geometry" title="Non-Euclidean geometry">non-Euclidean geometry</a> in the works of Bolyai, Lobachevsky, and Gauss (who never published his theory).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sommerville1919_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sommerville1919-105">[103]</a></sup> They demonstrated that ordinary <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_space" title="Euclidean space">Euclidean space</a> is only one possibility for development of geometry. A broad vision of the subject of geometry was then expressed  by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riemann" title="Riemann">Riemann</a> in his 1867 inauguration lecture <i>Über die Hypothesen, welche der Geometrie zu Grunde liegen</i> (<i>On the hypotheses on which geometry is based</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup> published only after his death. Riemann's new idea of space proved crucial in <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_relativity_theory" title="General relativity theory">general relativity theory</a>. <a href="/wiki/Riemannian_geometry" title="Riemannian geometry">Riemannian geometry</a>, which considers very general spaces in which the notion of length is defined, is a mainstay of modern geometry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pesic2007_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pesic2007-83">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Topology">Topology</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Topology" title="Topology">Topology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trefoil_knot_arb.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Trefoil_knot_arb.png/220px-Trefoil_knot_arb.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Trefoil_knot_arb.png/330px-Trefoil_knot_arb.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Trefoil_knot_arb.png/440px-Trefoil_knot_arb.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trefoil_knot_arb.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A thickening of the <a href="/wiki/Trefoil_knot" title="Trefoil knot">trefoil knot</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Topology" title="Topology">Topology</a> is the field concerned with the properties of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continuous_mapping" title="Continuous mapping">continuous mappings</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crossley2011_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crossley2011-107">[105]</a></sup> and can be considered a generalization of Euclidean geometry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NashSen1988_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-NashSen1988-108">[106]</a></sup> In practice, topology often means dealing with large-scale properties of spaces, such as <a href="/wiki/Connectedness" title="Connectedness">connectedness</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compact_(topology)" title="Compact (topology)">compactness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Munkres_51-4"><a href="#cite_note-Munkres-51">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>The field of topology, which saw massive development in the 20th century, is in a technical sense a type of <a href="/wiki/Transformation_geometry" title="Transformation geometry">transformation geometry</a>, in which transformations are <a href="/wiki/Homeomorphism" title="Homeomorphism">homeomorphisms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin1996_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martin1996-109">[107]</a></sup> This has often been expressed in the form of the saying 'topology is rubber-sheet geometry'. Subfields of topology include <a href="/wiki/Geometric_topology" title="Geometric topology">geometric topology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Differential_topology" title="Differential topology">differential topology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_topology" title="Algebraic topology">algebraic topology</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_topology" title="General topology">general topology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May1999_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-May1999-110">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algebraic_geometry">Algebraic geometry</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_geometry" title="Algebraic geometry">Algebraic geometry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Calabi_yau.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1493" data-file-width="1432" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Calabi_yau.jpg/220px-Calabi_yau.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Calabi_yau.jpg/330px-Calabi_yau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Calabi_yau.jpg/440px-Calabi_yau.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Calabi_yau.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Quintic <a href="/wiki/Calabi%E2%80%93Yau_manifold" title="Calabi–Yau manifold">Calabi–Yau threefold</a></div></div></div>
<p>The field of <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_geometry" title="Algebraic geometry">algebraic geometry</a> developed from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartesian_geometry" title="Cartesian geometry">Cartesian geometry</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Co-ordinates" title="Co-ordinates">co-ordinates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inc1905_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-inc1905-111">[109]</a></sup> It underwent periodic periods of growth, accompanied by the creation and study of <a href="/wiki/Projective_geometry" title="Projective geometry">projective geometry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Birational_geometry" title="Birational geometry">birational geometry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_variety" title="Algebraic variety">algebraic varieties</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Commutative_algebra" title="Commutative algebra">commutative algebra</a>, among other topics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dieudonne1985_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dieudonne1985-112">[110]</a></sup> From the late 1950s through the mid-1970s it had undergone major foundational development, largely due to work of <a href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Serre" title="Jean-Pierre Serre">Jean-Pierre Serre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Grothendieck" title="Alexander Grothendieck">Alexander Grothendieck</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dieudonne1985_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dieudonne1985-112">[110]</a></sup> This led to the introduction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scheme_(algebraic_geometry)" title="Scheme (algebraic geometry)">schemes</a> and greater emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_topology" title="Algebraic topology">topological</a> methods, including various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cohomology_theory" title="Cohomology theory">cohomology theories</a>.
